<p><b>News</b> is <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a> about current events. This may be provided through many different <a href="/wiki/Media_(communication)" title="Media (communication)">media</a>: <a href="/wiki/Word_of_mouth" title="Word of mouth">word of mouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mail" title="Mail">postal systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Broadcasting" title="Broadcasting">broadcasting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications" title="Telecommunications">electronic communication</a>, or through the testimony of <a href="/wiki/Witness" title="Witness">observers and witnesses</a> to events. News is sometimes called "<b>hard news</b>" to differentiate it from <a href="/wiki/Soft_media" title="Soft media">soft media</a>.
</p><p>Common topics for news reports include <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a>, <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>, <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">environment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economy" title="Economy">economy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">business</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fashion" title="Fashion">fashion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Entertainment" title="Entertainment">entertainment</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sport" title="Sport">sport</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Unusual_articles" title="Wikipedia:Unusual articles">quirky or unusual events</a>. Government proclamations, concerning <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">royal ceremonies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">laws</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">taxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">criminals</a>, have been dubbed news since ancient times. <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">Technological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_change" title="Social change">social developments</a>, often driven by government communication and <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">espionage</a> networks, have increased the speed with which news can spread, as well as influenced its content. The genre of news as we know it today is closely associated with the <a href="/wiki/Newspaper" title="Newspaper">newspaper</a>.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meaning">Meaning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Meaning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> word "news" developed in the 14th century as a special use of the plural form of "new". In <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a>, the equivalent word was <i>newes</i>, like the French <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nouvelles" title="wikt:nouvelles">nouvelles</a></i> and the German <i>Neues</i>. Similar developments are found in the <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic languages</a> – namely cognates from <a href="/wiki/Serbo-Croatian" title="Serbo-Croatian">Serbo-Croatian</a> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/novost" title="wikt:novost">novost</a></i> (from <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nov#Serbo-Croatian" title="wikt:nov">nov</a></i>, "new"), <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovak_language" title="Slovak language">Slovak</a> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/noviny" title="wikt:noviny">noviny</a></i> (from <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nov%C3%BD" title="wikt:nový">nový</a></i>, "new"), the <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> <i>nowiny</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a> <i>novini</i> and <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> <i>novosti</i> – and likewise in the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> <i>newyddion</i> (from <i>newydd</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a> <i>nowodhow</i> (from <i>nowydh</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jessica_Garretson_Finch" title="Jessica Garretson Finch">Jessica Garretson Finch</a> is credited with coining the phrase "current events" while teaching at <a href="/wiki/Barnard_College" title="Barnard College">Barnard College</a> in the 1890s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obituary_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-obituary-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Newness">Newness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Newness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As its name implies, "news" typically connotes the presentation of new information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephens13_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stephens13-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith7_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith7-5">[5]</a></sup> The newness of news gives it an uncertain quality which distinguishes it from the more careful investigations of history or other scholarly disciplines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith7_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith7-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pettegree3_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pettegree3-7">[7]</a></sup> Whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historians" title="Historians">historians</a> tend to view events as causally related manifestations of underlying processes, news stories tend to describe events in isolation, and to exclude discussion of the relationships between them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Park675_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Park675-8">[8]</a></sup> News conspicuously describes the world in the present or immediate past, even when the most important aspects of a news story have occurred long in the past—or are expected to occur in the future. To make the news, an ongoing process must have some "peg", an event in time which anchors it to the present moment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Park675_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Park675-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Relatedly, news often addresses aspects of reality which seem unusual, deviant, or out of the ordinary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Hence the famous dictum that "Dog Bites Man" is not news, but "Man Bites Dog" is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another corollary of the newness of news is that, as new technology enables new media to disseminate news more quickly, 'slower' forms of communication may move away from 'news' towards 'analysis'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commodity">Commodity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Commodity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to some theories, "news" is whatever the news industry sells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Journalism, broadly understood along the same lines, is the act or occupation of collecting and providing news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> From a commercial perspective, news is simply one input, along with paper (or an electronic server) necessary to prepare a final product for distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> A news agency supplies this resource "wholesale" and publishers enhance it for retail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Globalization6_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Globalization6-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacGregor_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacGregor-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tone">Tone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Tone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most purveyors of news value impartiality, neutrality, and <a href="/wiki/Journalistic_objectivity" title="Journalistic objectivity">objectivity</a>, despite the inherent difficulty of reporting without political bias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Perception of these values has changed greatly over time as sensationalized '<a href="/wiki/Tabloid_journalism" title="Tabloid journalism">tabloid journalism</a>' has risen in popularity. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Schudson" title="Michael Schudson">Michael Schudson</a> has argued that before the era of World War I and the concomitant rise of <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a>, journalists were not aware of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Media_bias" title="Media bias">bias in reporting</a>, let alone actively correcting for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> News is also sometimes said to portray the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a>, but this relationship is elusive and qualified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paradoxically, another property commonly attributed to news is <a href="/wiki/Sensationalism" title="Sensationalism">sensationalism</a>, the disproportionate focus on, and exaggeration of, emotive stories for public consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SKH_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-SKH-23">[23]</a></sup> This news is also not unrelated to <a href="/wiki/Gossip" title="Gossip">gossip</a>, the human practice of sharing information about other humans of mutual interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> A common sensational topic is violence; hence another news dictum, "if it bleeds, it leads".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Newsworthiness">Newsworthiness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Newsworthiness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Newsworthiness is defined as a subject having sufficient relevance to the public or a special audience to warrant press attention or coverage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>News values seem to be common across cultures. People seem to be interested in news to the extent which it has a big impact, describes conflicts, happens nearby, involves well-known people, and deviates from the norms of everyday happenings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> War is a common news topic, partly because it involves unknown events that could pose personal danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_telecommunication" title="History of telecommunication">History of telecommunication</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_news">Folk news</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Folk news">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Evidence suggests that cultures around the world have found a place for people to share stories about interesting new information. Among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zulus" title="Zulus">Zulus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongolians</a>, Polynesians, and American Southerners, anthropologists have documented the practice of questioning travelers for news as a matter of priority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Sufficiently important news would be repeated quickly and often, and could spread by word of mouth over a large geographic area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Even as printing presses came into use in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, news for the general public often travelled orally via monks, travelers, <a href="/wiki/Town_crier" title="Town crier">town criers</a>, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The news is also transmitted in public gathering places, such as the Greek forum and the Roman baths. Starting in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coffeehouses" title="Coffeehouses">coffeehouses</a> served as important sites for the spread of news, even after telecommunications became widely available. The history of the coffee houses is traced from Arab countries, which was introduced in England in the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In the Muslim world, people have gathered and exchanged news at mosques and other social places. Travelers on pilgrimages to Mecca traditionally stay at <a href="/wiki/Caravanserai" title="Caravanserai">caravanserais</a>, roadside inns, along the way, and these places have naturally served as hubs for gaining news of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> In late medieval Britain, reports ("tidings") of major events were a topic of great public interest, as chronicled in Chaucer's 1380 <i><a href="/wiki/The_House_of_Fame" title="The House of Fame">The House of Fame</a></i> and other works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_proclamations">Government proclamations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Government proclamations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fotothek_df_tg_0007253_St%C3%A4ndebuch_%5E_Beruf_%5E_Ausrufer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="820" data-file-width="624" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Fotothek_df_tg_0007253_St%C3%A4ndebuch_%5E_Beruf_%5E_Ausrufer.jpg/220px-Fotothek_df_tg_0007253_St%C3%A4ndebuch_%5E_Beruf_%5E_Ausrufer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Fotothek_df_tg_0007253_St%C3%A4ndebuch_%5E_Beruf_%5E_Ausrufer.jpg/330px-Fotothek_df_tg_0007253_St%C3%A4ndebuch_%5E_Beruf_%5E_Ausrufer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Fotothek_df_tg_0007253_St%C3%A4ndebuch_%5E_Beruf_%5E_Ausrufer.jpg/440px-Fotothek_df_tg_0007253_St%C3%A4ndebuch_%5E_Beruf_%5E_Ausrufer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fotothek_df_tg_0007253_St%C3%A4ndebuch_%5E_Beruf_%5E_Ausrufer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woodcut by <a href="/wiki/Tommaso_Garzoni" title="Tommaso Garzoni">Tommaso Garzoni</a> depicting a town crier with a trumpet</div></div></div>
<p>Before the invention of newspapers in the early 17th century, official government bulletins and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edicts" title="Edicts">edicts</a> were circulated at times in some centralized empires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The first documented use of an organized <a href="/wiki/Courier" title="Courier">courier</a> service for the diffusion of written documents is in Egypt, where Pharaohs used couriers for the diffusion of their decrees in the territory of the State (2400 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> regularly publicized his heroic deeds in Gaul, and upon becoming dictator of Rome began publishing government announcements called <i><a href="/wiki/Acta_Diurna" title="Acta Diurna">Acta Diurna</a></i>. These were carved in metal or stone and posted in public places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allan,_2004_p._9_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allan,_2004_p._9-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In medieval England, parliamentary declarations were delivered to <a href="/wiki/Reeve_(England)" title="Reeve (England)">sheriffs</a> for public display and reading at the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Specially sanctioned messengers have been recognized in <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Vietnam" title="Culture of Vietnam">Vietnamese culture</a>, among the <a href="/wiki/Khasi_people" title="Khasi people">Khasi people</a> in India, and in the <a href="/wiki/Meskwaki" title="Meskwaki">Fox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ho-Chunk" title="Ho-Chunk">Winnebago</a> cultures of the American midwest. The <a href="/wiki/Zulu_Kingdom" title="Zulu Kingdom">Zulu Kingdom</a> used runners to quickly disseminate news. In West Africa, news can be spread by <a href="/wiki/Griot" title="Griot">griots</a>. In most cases, the official spreaders of news have been closely aligned with holders of political power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Town_criers" title="Town criers">Town criers</a> were a common means of conveying information to citydwellers. In thirteenth-century Florence, criers known as <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">banditori</i></span> arrived in the market regularly, to announce political news, to convoke public meetings, and to call the populace to arms. In 1307 and 1322–1325, laws were established governing their appointment, conduct, and salary. These laws stipulated how many times a <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">banditoro</i></span> was to repeat a proclamation (forty) and where in the city they were to read them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Different declarations sometimes came with additional protocols; announcements regarding the plague were also to be read at the city gates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> These proclamations all used a standard format, beginning with an <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exordium_(rhetoric)" title="Exordium (rhetoric)">exordium</a></i>—"The worshipful and most esteemed gentlemen of the Eight of Ward and Security of the city of Florence make it known, notify, and expressly command, to whosoever, of whatever status, rank, quality and condition"—and continuing with a statement (<i>narratio</i>), a request made upon the listeners (<i>petitio</i>), and the penalty to be exacted from those who would not comply (<i>peroratio</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In addition to major declarations, <i>bandi</i> (announcements) might concern petty crimes, requests for information, and notices about missing slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Machiavelli" title="Niccolò Machiavelli">Niccolò Machiavelli</a> was captured by the Medicis in 1513, following a bando calling for his immediate surrender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Some town criers could be paid to include advertising along with news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, official messages were regularly distributed at mosques, by traveling holy men, and by secular criers. These criers were sent to read official announcements in marketplaces, highways, and other well-traveled places, sometimes issuing commands and penalties for disobedience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_news_networks">Early news networks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Early news networks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The spread of news has always been linked to the communications networks in place to disseminate it. Thus, political, religious, and commercial interests have historically controlled, expanded, and monitored communications channels by which news could spread. Postal services have long been closely entwined with the maintenance of political power in a large area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fang14_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fang14-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephens27_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stephens27-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the imperial communication channels, called the "<a href="/wiki/Royal_Road" title="Royal Road">Royal Road</a>" traversed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Empire" title="Assyrian Empire">Assyrian Empire</a> and served as a key source of its power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The Roman Empire maintained a vast network of roads, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Cursus_publicus" title="Cursus publicus">cursus publicus</a></i>, for similar purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Visible chains of long-distance signaling, known as <a href="/wiki/Optical_telegraph" title="Optical telegraph">optical telegraphy</a>, have also been used throughout history to convey limited types of information. These can have ranged from smoke and fire signals to advanced systems using semaphore codes and telescopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker13_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker13-53">[53]</a></sup> The latter form of optical telegraph came into use in Japan, Britain, France, and Germany from the 1790s through the 1850s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Distelrath45_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Distelrath45-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KaiYuanZaBaoRemake.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="258" data-file-width="339" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/KaiYuanZaBaoRemake.jpg/220px-KaiYuanZaBaoRemake.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/KaiYuanZaBaoRemake.jpg/330px-KaiYuanZaBaoRemake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a3/KaiYuanZaBaoRemake.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KaiYuanZaBaoRemake.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reproduction of Kaiyuan Za Bao court newspaper from the Tang dynasty</div></div></div>
<p>The world's first written news may have originated in <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">eighth century BCE China</a>, where reports gathered by officials were eventually compiled as the <i><a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_Annals" title="Spring and Autumn Annals">Spring and Autumn Annals</a></i>. The annals, whose compilation is attributed to <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a>, were available to a sizeable reading public and dealt with common news themes—though they straddle the line between news and history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> is credited with developing one of the most effective imperial surveillance and communications networks in the ancient world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Government-produced news sheets, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tipao" title="Tipao">tipao</a>, circulated among court officials during the late Han dynasty (second and third centuries AD). Between 713 and 734, the <i><a href="/wiki/Kaiyuan_Za_Bao" title="Kaiyuan Za Bao">Kaiyuan Za Bao</a></i> ("Bulletin of the Court") of the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> published government news; it was handwritten on silk and read by government officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith14_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith14-58">[58]</a></sup> The court created a Bureau of Official Reports (<i>Jin Zhouyuan</i>) to centralize news distribution for the court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Newsletters called <i>ch'ao pao</i> continued to be produced and gained wider public circulation in the following centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith14a_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith14a-60">[60]</a></sup> In 1582 there was the first reference to privately published newssheets in Beijing, during the late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brook_xxi_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-brook_xxi-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Japan had effective communications and postal delivery networks at several points in its history, first in 646 with the <a href="/wiki/Taika_Reform" title="Taika Reform">Taika Reform</a> and again during the <a href="/wiki/Kamakura_period" title="Kamakura period">Kamakura period</a> from 1183 to 1333. The system depended on <i><a href="/wiki/Hikyaku" title="Hikyaku">hikyaku</a></i>, runners, and regularly spaced relay stations. By this method, news could travel between Kyoto and Kamakura in 5–7 days. Special horse-mounted messengers could move information at the speed of 170 kilometers per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Distelrath45_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Distelrath45-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The Japanese shogunates were less tolerant than the Chinese government of news circulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith14_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith14-58">[58]</a></sup> The postal system established during the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> was even more effective, with average speeds of 125–150 km/day and express speed of 200 km/day. This system was initially used only by the government, taking private communications only at exorbitant prices. Private services emerged and in 1668 established their own <i><a href="/wiki/Kabunakama" title="Kabunakama">nakama</a></i> (guild). They became even faster, and created an effective optical telegraphy system using flags by day and lanterns and mirrors by night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Distelrath45_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Distelrath45-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Europe, during the Middle Ages, elites relied on runners to transmit news over long distances. At 33 kilometres per day, a runner would take two months to bring a message across the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> from Bruges to Riga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Europe" title="Early modern Europe">early modern period</a>, increased cross-border interaction created a rising need for information which was met by concise handwritten newssheets. The driving force of this new development was the commercial advantage provided by up-to-date news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pettegree3_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pettegree3-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1556, the government of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a> first published the monthly <i>Notizie scritte</i>, which cost one <a href="/wiki/Gazette" title="Gazette">gazetta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WAN_timeline_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-WAN_timeline-67">[67]</a></sup> These <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avviso" title="Avviso">avvisi</a> were handwritten newsletters and used to convey political, military, and economic news quickly and efficiently to Italian cities (1500–1700)—sharing some characteristics of newspapers though usually not considered true newspapers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> <i>Avvisi</i> were sold by subscription under the auspices of military, religious, and banking authorities. Sponsorship flavored the contents of each series, which were circulated under many different names. Subscribers included clerics, diplomatic staff, and noble families. By the last quarter of the seventeenth century, long passages from <i>avvisi</i> were finding their way into published monthlies such as the <a href="/wiki/Mercure_de_France" title="Mercure de France">Mercure de France</a> and, in northern Italy, <i>Pallade veneta</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Postkurse_1563.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2297" data-file-width="3205" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Postkurse_1563.jpg/220px-Postkurse_1563.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Postkurse_1563.jpg/330px-Postkurse_1563.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Postkurse_1563.jpg/440px-Postkurse_1563.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Postkurse_1563.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some European postal routes in 1563</div></div></div>
<p>Postal services enabled merchants and monarchs to stay abreast of important information. For the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Maximilian I, Holy Roman Emperor">Maximillian I</a> in 1490 authorized two brothers from the Italian Tasso family, Francesco and Janetto, to create a network of courier stations linked by riders. They began with a communications line between Innsbruck and Mechelen and grew from there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LampePloeckl248_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-LampePloeckl248-72">[72]</a></sup> In 1505 this network expanded to Spain, new governed by Maximilian's son <a href="/wiki/Philip_I_of_Castile" title="Philip I of Castile">Philip</a>. These riders could travel 180 kilometers in a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> This system became the <a href="/wiki/Reichspost" title="Reichspost">Imperial Reichspost</a>, administered by Tasso descendants (subsequently known as <a href="/wiki/Lamoral_II_Claudius_Franz,_Count_of_Thurn_and_Taxis" title="Lamoral II Claudius Franz, Count of Thurn and Taxis">Thurn-und-Taxis</a>), who in 1587 received exclusive operating rights from the Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LampePloeckl248_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-LampePloeckl248-72">[72]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/La_Poste_(France)" title="La Poste (France)">French postal service</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Post_Office" title="General Post Office">English postal service</a> also began at this time, but did not become comprehensive until the early 1600s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LampePloeckl248_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-LampePloeckl248-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In 1620, the English system linked with Thurn-und-Taxis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker13_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker13-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>These connections underpinned an extensive system of news circulation, with handwritten items bearing dates and places of origin. Centred in Germany, the network took in news from Russia, the Balkans, Italy, Britain, France, and the Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The German lawyer <a href="/wiki/Christoph_von_Scheurl" title="Christoph von Scheurl">Christoph von Scheurl</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fugger" title="Fugger">Fugger</a> house of Augsburg were prominent hubs in this network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker11_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker11-77">[77]</a></sup> Letters describing historically significant events could gain wide circulation as news reports. Indeed, personal correspondence sometimes acted only as a convenient channel through which news could flow across a larger network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> A common type of business communication was a simple listing of current prices, the circulation of which quickened the flow of international trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker10_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker10-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Businesspeople also wanted to know about events related to shipping, the affairs of other businesses, and political developments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker10_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker10-79">[79]</a></sup> Even after the advent of international newspapers, business owners still valued correspondence highly as a source of reliable news that would affect their enterprise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Handwritten newsletters, which could be produced quickly for a limited clientele, also continued into the 1600s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker11_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker11-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_the_newspaper">Rise of the newspaper</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Rise of the newspaper">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_London_Gazette_28314.pdf"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1731" data-file-width="1060" decoding="async" height="359" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/The_London_Gazette_28314.pdf/page1-220px-The_London_Gazette_28314.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/The_London_Gazette_28314.pdf/page1-330px-The_London_Gazette_28314.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/The_London_Gazette_28314.pdf/page1-440px-The_London_Gazette_28314.pdf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_London_Gazette_28314.pdf" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The London Gazette</i>, "Published By Authority" (of the <a href="/wiki/Worshipful_Company_of_Stationers_and_Newspaper_Makers" title="Worshipful Company of Stationers and Newspaper Makers">Stationers' Company</a>) on 3 December 1909</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_newspapers_and_magazines" title="History of newspapers and magazines">History of newspapers and magazines</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_paper" title="History of paper">spread of paper</a> and the <a href="/wiki/History_of_printing" title="History of printing">printing press</a> from China to Europe preceded a major advance in the transmission of news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> With the spread of printing presses and the creation of new markets in the 1500s, news underwent a shift from factual and precise economic reporting, to a more emotive and freewheeling format. (Private newsletters containing important intelligence therefore remained in use by people who needed to know.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_oldest_newspapers" title="List of the oldest newspapers">first newspapers</a> emerged in Germany in the early 1600s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relation_aller_F%C3%BCrnemmen_und_gedenckw%C3%BCrdigen_Historien" title="Relation aller Fürnemmen und gedenckwürdigen Historien">Relation aller Fürnemmen und gedenckwürdigen Historien</a>, from 1605, is recognized as the world's first formalized 'newspaper';<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Relation_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Relation-85">[85]</a></sup> while not a 'newspaper' in the modern sense, the Ancient Roman <i><a href="/wiki/Acta_Diurna" title="Acta Diurna">Acta Diurna</a></i> served a similar purpose circa 131 BC.
</p><p>The new format, which mashed together numerous unrelated and perhaps dubious reports from far-flung locations, created a radically new and jarring experience for its readers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> A variety of styles emerged, from single-story tales, to compilations, overviews, and personal and impersonal types of news analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>News for public consumption was at first tightly controlled by governments. By 1530, England had created a licensing system for the press and banned "seditious opinions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Under the <a href="/wiki/Licensing_Order_of_1643" title="Licensing Order of 1643">Licensing Act</a>, publication was restricted to approved presses—as exemplified by The London Gazette, which prominently bore the words: "Published By Authority".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Parliament allowed the Licensing Act to lapse in 1695, beginning a new era marked by <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whig</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tory_(British_political_grouping)" title="Tory (British political grouping)">Tory</a> newspapers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> (During this era, the <a href="/wiki/Stamp_Act_1712" title="Stamp Act 1712">Stamp Act</a> limited newspaper distribution simply by making them expensive to sell and buy.) In France, censorship was even more constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Consequently, many Europeans read newspapers originating from beyond their national borders—especially from the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a>, where publishers could evade state censorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The new United States saw a newspaper boom beginning with the Revolutionary era, accelerated by spirited debates over the establishment of a new government, spurred on by subsidies contained in the 1792 <a href="/wiki/Postal_Service_Act" title="Postal Service Act">Postal Service Act</a>, and continuing into the 1800s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> American newspapers got many of their stories by copying reports from each other. Thus by offering free postage to newspapers wishing to exchange copies, the Postal Service Act subsidized a rapidly growing news network through which different stories could percolate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Newspapers thrived during the colonization of the <a href="/wiki/American_frontier" title="American frontier">West</a>, fueled by high literacy and a newspaper-loving culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> By 1880, San Francisco rivaled New York in number of different newspapers and in printed newspaper copies per capita.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Boosterism" title="Boosterism">Boosters</a> of new towns felt that newspapers covering local events brought legitimacy, recognition, and community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The 1830s American, wrote <a href="/wiki/Alexis_de_Tocqueville" title="Alexis de Tocqueville">Alexis de Tocqueville</a>, was "a very civilized man prepared for a time to face life in the forest, plunging into the wilderness of the New World with his Bible, ax, and newspapers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> In France, the Revolution brought forth an abundance of newspapers and a new climate of press freedom, followed by a return to repression under Napoleon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> In 1792 the Revolutionaries set up a news ministry called the <i>Bureau d'Esprit</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some newspapers published in the 1800s and after retained the commercial orientation characteristic of the private newsletters of the Renaissance. Economically oriented newspapers published new types of data enabled the advent of <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Economic_statistics" title="Economic statistics">economic statistics</a> which could inform sophisticated investment decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> These newspapers, too, became available for larger sections of society, not just elites, keen on investing some of their savings in the <a href="/wiki/Stock_market" title="Stock market">stock markets</a>. Yet, as in the case other newspapers, the incorporation of advertising into the newspaper led to justified reservations about accepting newspaper information at face value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Economic newspapers also became promoters of economic ideologies, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keynesianism" title="Keynesianism">Keynesianism</a> in the mid-1900s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Newspapers came to sub-Saharan Africa via colonization. The first English-language newspaper in the area was <i>The Royal Gazette and Sierra Leone Advertiser</i>, established in 1801, and followed by <i>The Royal Gold Coast Gazette and Commercial Intelligencer</i> in 1822 and the <i>Liberia Herald</i> in 1826.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> A number of nineteenth-century African newspapers were established by missionaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fosu60_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fosu60-106">[106]</a></sup> These newspapers by and large promoted the colonial governments and served the interests of European settlers by relaying news from Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fosu60_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fosu60-106">[106]</a></sup> The first newspaper published in a native African language was the <i>Muigwithania</i>, published in Kikuyu by the Kenyan Central Association.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fosu60_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fosu60-106">[106]</a></sup> <i>Muigwithania</i> and other newspapers published by indigenous Africans took strong opposition stances, agitating strongly for African independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Newspapers were censored heavily during the colonial period—as well as after formal independence. Some liberalization and diversification took place in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Newspapers were slow to <a href="/wiki/Arab_culture" title="Arab culture">spread to the Arab world</a>, which had a stronger tradition of <a href="/wiki/Communication#Verbal_communication" title="Communication">oral communication</a>, and mistrust of the European approach to news reporting. By the end of the eighteenth century, the Ottoman Empire's leaders in Istanbul monitored the European press, but its contents were not disseminated for mass consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Some of the first written news in modern North Africa arose in Egypt under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali</a>, who developed the local paper industry and initiated the limited circulation of news bulletins called <span title="Arabic-language text"><i lang="ar">jurnals</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Beginning in the 1850s and 1860s, the private press began to develop in the multi-religious country of Lebanon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Newswire">Newswire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Newswire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The development of the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_telegraph" title="Electrical telegraph">electrical telegraph</a>, which often travelled along railroad lines, enabled news to travel faster, over longer distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> (Days before Morse's Baltimore–Washington line transmitted the famous question, "What hath God wrought?", it transmitted the news that Henry Clay and Theodore Frelinghuysen had been chosen by the Whig nominating party.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allan,_2004_p._9_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allan,_2004_p._9-37">[37]</a></sup> Telegraph networks enabled a new centralization of the news, in the hands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wire_services" title="Wire services">wire services</a> concentrated in major cities. The modern form of these originated with <a href="/wiki/Charles-Louis_Havas" title="Charles-Louis Havas">Charles-Louis Havas</a>, who founded Bureau Havas (later <a href="/wiki/Agence_France-Presse" title="Agence France-Presse">Agence France-Presse</a>) in Paris. Havas began in 1832, using the French government's optical telegraph network. In 1840 he began using pigeons for communications to Paris, London, and Brussels. Havas began to use the electric telegraph when it became available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of Havas's proteges, <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Wolff" title="Bernhard Wolff">Bernhard Wolff</a>, founded <a href="/wiki/Wolffs_Telegraphisches_Bureau" title="Wolffs Telegraphisches Bureau">Wolffs Telegraphisches Bureau</a> in Berlin in 1849.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Another Havas disciple, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Reuter" title="Paul Reuter">Paul Reuter</a>, began collecting news from Germany and France in 1849, and in 1851 immigrated to London, where he established the <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a> news agency—specializing in news from the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In 1863, William Saunders and Edward Spender formed the <a href="/wiki/Central_News_Agency_(London)" title="Central News Agency (London)">Central Press</a> agency, later called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Press_Association" title="Press Association">Press Association</a>, to handle domestic news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Just before insulated telegraph line crossed the English Channel in 1851, Reuter won the right to transmit stock exchange prices between Paris and London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wenzlhuemer90_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wenzlhuemer90-117">[117]</a></sup> He maneuvered Reuters into a dominant global position with the motto "Follow the Cable", setting up news outposts across the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> in Alexandria (1865), Bombay (1866), Melbourne (1874), Sydney (1874), and Cape Town (1876).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wenzlhuemer90_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wenzlhuemer90-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> became a news powerhouse, gaining a lead position through an exclusive arrangement with the <a href="/wiki/Western_Union" title="Western Union">Western Union</a> company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starr177_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starr177-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>The telegraph ushered in a new global communications regime, accompanied by a restructuring of the national postal systems, and closely followed by the advent of telephone lines. With the value of international news at a premium, governments, businesses, and news agencies moved aggressively to reduce transmission times. In 1865, Reuters had the scoop on the <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Assassination of Abraham Lincoln">Lincoln assassination</a>, reporting the news in England twelve days after the event took place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> In 1866, <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_telegraph_cable" title="Transatlantic telegraph cable">an undersea telegraph cable</a> successfully connected Ireland to Newfoundland (and thus the Western Union network) cutting trans-Atlantic transmission time from days to hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allan,_2004_p._17_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allan,_2004_p._17-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> The transatlantic cable allowed fast exchange of information about the London and New York stock exchanges, as well as the New York, Chicago, and Liverpool commodity exchanges—for the price of $5–10, in gold, per word.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Transmitting On 11 May 1857, a young British telegraph operator in Delhi signaled home to alert the authorities of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">Indian Rebellion of 1857</a>. The rebels proceeded to disrupt the British telegraph network, which was rebuilt with more redundancies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> In 1902–1903, Britain and the U.S. completed the circumtelegraphy of the planet with transpacific cables from Canada to Fiji and New Zealand (British Empire), and from the US to Hawaii and the occupied Philippines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> U.S. reassertions of the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a> notwithstanding, Latin America was a battleground of competing telegraphic interests until World War I, after which U.S. interests finally did consolidate their power in the hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eisenbahnen-_und_Telegraphendichte_der_Erde_um_1900.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="858" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Eisenbahnen-_und_Telegraphendichte_der_Erde_um_1900.jpg/220px-Eisenbahnen-_und_Telegraphendichte_der_Erde_um_1900.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Eisenbahnen-_und_Telegraphendichte_der_Erde_um_1900.jpg/330px-Eisenbahnen-_und_Telegraphendichte_der_Erde_um_1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Eisenbahnen-_und_Telegraphendichte_der_Erde_um_1900.jpg/440px-Eisenbahnen-_und_Telegraphendichte_der_Erde_um_1900.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eisenbahnen-_und_Telegraphendichte_der_Erde_um_1900.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World railway and telegraph system, 1900</div></div></div>
<p>By the turn of the century (i.e., circa 1900), Wolff, Havas, and Reuters formed a news cartel, dividing up the global market into three sections, in which each had more-or-less exclusive distribution rights and relationships with national agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Each agency's area corresponded roughly to the colonial sphere of its mother country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Reuters and the Australian national news service had an agreement to exchange news only with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Due to the high cost of maintaining infrastructure, political goodwill, and global reach, newcomers found it virtually impossible to challenge the big three European agencies or the American Associated Press.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> In 1890 Reuters (in partnership with the Press Association, England's major news agency for domestic stories) expanded into "soft" news stories for public consumption, about topics such as sports and "human interest".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> In 1904, the big three wire services opened relations with <i>Vestnik</i>, the news agency of Czarist Russia, to their group, though they maintained their own reporters in Moscow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> During and after the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>, the outside agencies maintained a working relationship with the Petrograd Telegraph Agency, renamed the Russian Telegraph Agency (ROSTA) and eventually the <a href="/wiki/TASS" title="TASS">Telegraph Agency of the Soviet Union (TASS)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palmer184_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer184-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chinese Communist Party">Chinese Communist Party</a> created its news agency, the Red China News Agency, in 1931; its primary responsibilities were the <i>Red China</i> newspaper and the internal <i>Reference News</i>. In 1937, the Party renamed the agency <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xinhua" title="Xinhua">Xinhua</a></i>, New China. Xinhua became the official news agency of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a> in 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XinXin_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-XinXin-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>These agencies touted their ability to distill events into "minute globules of news", 20–30 word summaries which conveyed the essence of new developments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palmer184_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer184-134">[134]</a></sup> Unlike newspapers, and contrary to the sentiments of some of their reporters, the agencies sought to keep their reports simple and factual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The wire services brought forth the "inverted pyramid" model of news copy, in which key facts appear at the start of the text, and more and more details are included as it goes along.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allan,_2004_p._17_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allan,_2004_p._17-121">[121]</a></sup> The sparse telegraphic writing style spilled over into newspapers, which often reprinted stories from the wire with little embellishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacGregor_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacGregor-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> In a 20 September 1918 <i>Pravda</i> editorial, Lenin instructed the Soviet press to cut back on their political rambling and produce many short anticapitalist news items in "telegraph style".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>As in previous eras, the news agencies provided special services to political and business clients, and these services constituted a significant portion of their operations and income. The wire services maintained close relationships with their respective national governments, which provided both press releases and payments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OBB23_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-OBB23-139">[139]</a></sup> The acceleration and centralization of economic news facilitated regional economic integration and <a href="/wiki/Economic_globalization" title="Economic globalization">economic globalization</a>. "It was the decrease in information costs and the increasing communication speed that stood at the roots of increased market integration, rather than falling transport costs by itself. In order to send goods to another area, merchants needed to know first whether in fact to send off the goods and to what place. Information costs and speed were essential for these decisions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_and_television">Radio and television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Radio and television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/British_Broadcasting_Company" title="British Broadcasting Company">British Broadcasting Company</a> began transmitting radio news from London in 1922, dependent entirely, by law, on the British news agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen26_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen26-141">[141]</a></sup> BBC radio marketed itself as a news by and for social elites, and hired only broadcasters who spoke with upper-class accents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The BBC gained importance in the May 1926 general strike, during which newspapers were closed and the radio served as the only source of news for an uncertain public. (To the displeasure of many listeners, the BBC took an unambiguously pro-government stance against the strikers).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen26_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allen26-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the US, RCA's Radio Group established its radio network, NBC, in 1926. The Paley family founded CBS soon after. These two networks, which supplied news broadcasts to subsidiaries and affiliates, dominated the airwaves throughout the period of radio's hegemony as a news source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Radio broadcasters in the United States negotiated a similar arrangement with the press in 1933, when they agreed to use only news from the Press–Radio Bureau and eschew advertising; this agreement soon collapsed and radio stations began reporting their own news (with advertising).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> As in Britain, American news radio avoided "controversial" topics as per norms established by the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Broadcasters" title="National Association of Broadcasters">National Association of Broadcasters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen34_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen34-146">[146]</a></sup> By 1939, 58% of Americans surveyed by <i>Fortune</i> considered radio news more accurate than newspapers, and 70% chose radio as their main news source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen34_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allen34-146">[146]</a></sup> Radio expanded rapidly across the continent, from 30 stations in 1920 to a thousand in the 1930s. This operation was financed mostly with advertising and public relations money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Soviet Union began a major international broadcasting operation in 1929, with stations in German, English and French. The <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> made use of the radio in its rise to power in Germany, with much of its propaganda focused on attacking the Soviet Bolsheviks. The British and Italian foreign radio services competed for influence in North Africa. All four of these broadcast services grew increasingly vitriolic as the European nations prepared for war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>The war provided an opportunity to expand radio and take advantage of its new potential. The BBC reported on <a href="/wiki/Normandy_landings" title="Normandy landings">Allied invasion of Normandy</a> on 8:00 a.m. of the morning it took place, and including a clip from German radio coverage of the same event. Listeners followed along with developments throughout the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The U.S. set up its <a href="/wiki/United_States_Office_of_War_Information" title="United States Office of War Information">Office of War Information</a> which by 1942 sent programming across South America, the Middle East, and East Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_Luxembourg_(English)" title="Radio Luxembourg (English)">Radio Luxembourg</a>, a centrally located high-power station on the continent, was <a href="/wiki/Germany_Calling" title="Germany Calling">seized by Germany</a>, and then <a href="/wiki/Radio_1212" title="Radio 1212">by the United States</a>—which created fake news programs appearing as though they were created by Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Targeting American troops in the Pacific, the Japanese government broadcast the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Zero_Hour_(World_War_II)" title="The Zero Hour (World War II)">Zero Hour</a>" program, which included news from the U.S. to make the soldiers homesick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> But by the end of the war, Britain had the largest radio network in the world, broadcasting internationally in 43 different languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Its scope would eventually be surpassed (by 1955) by the worldwide <a href="/wiki/Voice_of_America" title="Voice of America">Voice of America</a> programs, produced by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Information_Agency" title="United States Information Agency">United States Information Agency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Britain and the United States, television news watching rose dramatically in the 1950s and by the 1960s supplanted radio as the public's primary source of news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> In the U.S., television was run by the same networks which owned radio: CBS, NBC, and an NBC spin-off called ABC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_R._Murrow" title="Edward R. Murrow">Edward R. Murrow</a>, who first entered the public ear as a war reporter in London, made the big leap to television to become an iconic newsman on CBS (and later the director of the United States Information Agency).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ted_Turner" title="Ted Turner">Ted Turner</a>'s creation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cable_News_Network" title="Cable News Network">Cable News Network</a> (CNN) in 1980 inaugurated a new era of <a href="/wiki/24-hour_news_cycle" title="24-hour news cycle">24-hour</a> satellite news broadcasting. In 1991, the BBC introduced a competitor, <a href="/wiki/BBC_World_Service_Television" title="BBC World Service Television">BBC World Service Television</a>. Rupert Murdoch's Australian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/News_Corporation_(1980%E2%80%932013)" title="News Corporation (1980–2013)">News Corporation</a> entered the picture with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fox_News_Channel" title="Fox News Channel">Fox News Channel</a> in the US, <a href="/wiki/Sky_News" title="Sky News">Sky News</a> in Britain, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fox_Networks_Group_Asia_Pacific" title="Fox Networks Group Asia Pacific">STAR TV</a> in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McNair108_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-McNair108-158">[158]</a></sup> Combining this new apparatus with the use of <a href="/wiki/Embedded_journalism" title="Embedded journalism">embedded reporters</a>, the United States waged the 1991–1992 <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a> with the assistance of nonstop <a href="/wiki/Media_coverage_of_the_Gulf_War" title="Media coverage of the Gulf War">media coverage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> CNN's specialty is the <a href="/wiki/Crisis" title="Crisis">crisis</a>, to which the network is prepared to shift its total attention if so chosen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HachtenCNN_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-HachtenCNN-160">[160]</a></sup> CNN news was transmitted via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/INTELSAT" title="INTELSAT">INTELSAT</a> communications satellites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> CNN, said an executive, would bring a "town crier to the global village".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomlinson_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tomlinson-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1996, the Qatar-owned broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Al_Jazeera" title="Al Jazeera">Al Jazeera</a> emerged as a powerful alternative to the Western media, capitalizing in part on anger in the Arab &amp; Muslim world regarding biased coverage of the Gulf War. Al Jazeera hired many news workers conveniently laid off by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BBC_Arabic_Television" title="BBC Arabic Television">BBC Arabic Television</a>, which closed in April 1996. It used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabsat" title="Arabsat">Arabsat</a> to broadcast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McNair108_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-McNair108-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internet">Internet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Internet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The early internet, known as <a href="/wiki/ARPANET" title="ARPANET">ARPANET</a>, was controlled by the U.S. Department of Defense and used mostly by academics. It became available to a wider public with the release of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Netscape_(browser)" title="Netscape (browser)">Netscape browser</a> in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> At first, news websites were mostly archives of print publications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> An early <a href="/wiki/Online_newspaper" title="Online newspaper">online newspaper</a> was the <i>Electronic Telegraph</i>, published by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/1994_Northridge_earthquake" title="1994 Northridge earthquake">A 1994 earthquake in California</a> was one of the first big stories to be reported online in real time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allan175_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allan175-167">[167]</a></sup> The new availability of web browsing made news sites accessible to more people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allan175_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allan175-167">[167]</a></sup> On the day of the <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City_bombing" title="Oklahoma City bombing">Oklahoma City bombing</a> in April 1995, people flocked to newsgroups and chatrooms to discuss the situation and share information. The <i>Oklahoma City Daily</i> posted news to its site within hours. Two of the only news sites capable of hosting images, the <i>San Jose Mercury News</i> and <i>Time</i> magazine, posted photographs of the scene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allan175_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-Allan175-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>Quantitatively, the internet has massively expanded the sheer volume of news items available to one person. The speed of news flow to individuals has also reached a new plateau.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> This insurmountable flow of news can daunt people and cause <a href="/wiki/Information_overload" title="Information overload">information overload</a>. <a href="/wiki/Zbigniew_Brzezinski" title="Zbigniew Brzezinski">Zbigniew Brzezinski</a> called this period the "technetronic era", in which "global reality increasingly absorbs the individual, involves him, and even occasionally overwhelms him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>In cases of government crackdowns or revolutions, the Internet has often become a major communication channel for news propagation; while it's a (relatively) simple act to shut down a newspaper, radio or television station, mobile devices such as smartphones and netbooks are much harder to detect and confiscate. The propagation of internet-capable mobile devices has also given rise to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citizen_journalist" title="Citizen journalist">citizen journalist</a>, who provide an additional perspective on unfolding events.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="News_media_today">News media today</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: News media today">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/News_media" title="News media">News media</a></div>
<p>News can travel through different <a href="/wiki/Media_(communication)" title="Media (communication)">communication media</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Globalization6_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Globalization6-17">[17]</a></sup> In modern times, printed news had to be phoned into a newsroom or brought there by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reporter" title="Reporter">reporter</a>, where it was typed and either transmitted over wire services or <a href="/wiki/Editing" title="Editing">edited</a> and manually <a href="/wiki/Typesetting" title="Typesetting">set in type</a> along with other news stories for a specific edition. Today, the term "<a href="/wiki/Breaking_news" title="Breaking news">breaking news</a>" has become trite as <a href="/wiki/Commercial_broadcasting" title="Commercial broadcasting">commercial broadcasting</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_cable_news" title="United States cable news">United States cable news</a> services that are available 24 hours a day use live <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">communications satellite</a> technology to bring current events into <a href="/wiki/Consumer" title="Consumer">consumers</a>' homes as the event occurs. Events that used to take hours or days to become common knowledge in towns or in nations are fed instantaneously to consumers via <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">mobile phone</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">internet</a>.
</p><p>Speed of news transmission, of course, still varies wildly on the basis of where and how one lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SilverblattZlobin42_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-SilverblattZlobin42-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Newspaper">Newspaper</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Newspaper">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NYTimes-Page1-11-11-1918.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3806" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/NYTimes-Page1-11-11-1918.jpg/220px-NYTimes-Page1-11-11-1918.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/NYTimes-Page1-11-11-1918.jpg/330px-NYTimes-Page1-11-11-1918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/NYTimes-Page1-11-11-1918.jpg/440px-NYTimes-Page1-11-11-1918.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NYTimes-Page1-11-11-1918.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A newspaper is one of the most common ways to receive the latest news.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Newspaper" title="Newspaper">Newspaper</a></div>
<p>Most large cities in the United States historically had morning and afternoon newspapers. With the addition of new communications media, afternoon newspapers have shut down and morning newspapers have lost circulation. Weekly newspapers have somewhat increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> In more and more cities, newspapers have established local market monopolies—i.e., a single newspaper is the only one in town. This process has accelerated since the 1980s, commensurate with a general trend of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consolidation_of_media_ownership" title="Consolidation of media ownership">consolidation in media ownership</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> In China, too, newspapers have gained exclusive status, city-by-city, and pooled into large associations such as Chengdu Business News. These associations function like news agencies, challenging the hegemony of Xinhua as a news provider.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XinXin_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-XinXin-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>The world's top three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_the_world_by_circulation" title="List of newspapers in the world by circulation">most circulated newspapers</a> all publish from Japan.
