</p><p>About one-third of newspaper revenue comes from sales; the majority comes from advertising.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Newspapers have struggled to maintain revenue given declining circulation and the free flow of information over the internet; some have implemented <a href="/wiki/Paywall" title="Paywall">paywalls</a> for their websites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the U.S., many newspapers have shifted their operations online, publishing around the clock rather than daily in order to keep pace with the internet society. Prognosticators have suggested that print newspapers will vanish from the U.S. in 5–20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_165-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-165">[165]</a></sup> Many newspapers have started to track social media engagement for trending news stories to cover. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Internationally distributed news channels include <a href="/wiki/BBC_News" title="BBC News">BBC News</a>, <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox News</a>, <a href="/wiki/MSNBC" title="MSNBC">MSNBC</a>, <a href="/wiki/CBC_News_Network" title="CBC News Network">CBC News Network</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sky_News" title="Sky News">Sky News</a>. Televisions are densely concentrated in the United States (98% of households), and the average American watches 4 hours of television programming each day. In other parts of the world, such as Kenya—especially rural areas without much electricity—televisions are rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SilverblattZlobin42_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-SilverblattZlobin42-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest supplier of international video news is Reuters TV, with 409 subscribers in 83 countries, 38 bureaus, and a reported audience of 1.5 billion people each day. The other major video news service is <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press_Television_News" title="Associated Press Television News">Associated Press Television News</a>. These two major agencies have agreements to exchange video news with ABC, NBC, CBS, CNN, and <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_(network)" title="Eurovision (network)">Eurovision</a>—itself a sizeable video news exchange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hachten39_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hachten39-174">[174]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/CNN_International" title="CNN International">CNN International</a> is a notable broadcaster in times of crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HachtenCNN_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-HachtenCNN-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internet_2">Internet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Internet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Online_journalism" title="Online journalism">Online journalism</a> is news that is reported on the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">internet</a>. News can be delivered more quickly through this method of news as well as accessed more easily. The internet era has transformed the understanding of news. Because the internet allows communication which is not only instantaneous, but also bi- or multi-directional, it has blurred the boundaries of who is a legitimate news producer. A common type of internet journalism is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blogging" title="Blogging">blogging</a>, which is a service of persistently written articles uploaded and written by one or more individuals. Millions of people in countries such as the United States and South Korea have taken up blogging. Many blogs have rather small audiences; some blogs are read by millions each month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> Social media sites, especially Twitter and Facebook, have become an important source of breaking news information and for disseminating links to news websites. Twitter declared in 2012: "It's like being delivered a newspaper whose headlines you'll always find interesting—you can discover news as it's happening, learn more about topics that are important to you, and get the inside scoop in real time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Cell phone cameras have normalized citizen <a href="/wiki/Photojournalism" title="Photojournalism">photojournalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Schudson" title="Michael Schudson">Michael Schudson</a>, professor at the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_Graduate_School_of_Journalism" title="Columbia University Graduate School of Journalism">Columbia University Graduate School of Journalism</a>, has said that "[e]verything we thought we once knew about journalism needs to be rethought in the Digital Age."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schudson_2011_205_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schudson_2011_205-178">[178]</a></sup> Today the work of journalism can be done from anywhere and done well. It requires no more than a reporter and a laptop. In that way, journalistic authority seems to have become more individual- and less institution-based. But does the individual reporter always have to be an actual journalist? Or can journalistic work be done from anywhere and by anyone? These are questions that refer to the core of journalistic practice and the definition of "news" itself. As Schudson has given emphasis to, the answer is not easily found; "the ground journalists walk upon is shaking, and the experience for both those who work in the field and those on the outside studying it is dizzying".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schudson_2011_205_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schudson_2011_205-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>Schudson has identified the following six specific areas where the ecology of news in his opinion has changed:
</p>
<ul><li>The line between the reader and writer has blurred.</li>
<li>The distinction among tweet, blog post, Facebook, newspaper story, magazine article, and book has blurred.</li>
<li>The line between professionals and amateurs has blurred, and a variety of "pro-am" relationships has emerged.</li>
<li>The boundaries delineating for-profit, public, and non-profit media have blurred, and the cooperation across these models of financing has developed.</li>
<li>Within commercial news organizations, the line between the news room and the business office has blurred.</li>
<li>The line between old media and new media has blurred, practically beyond recognition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>These alterations inevitably have fundamental ramifications for the contemporary ecology of news. "The boundaries of journalism, which just a few years ago seemed relatively clear, and permanent, have become less distinct, and this blurring, while potentially the foundation of progress even as it is the source of risk, has given rise to a new set of journalistic principles and practices",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Schudson puts it. It is indeed complex, but it seems to be the future.
</p><p>Online news has also changed the geographic reach of individual news stories, diffusing readership from city-by-city markets to a potentially global audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_165-3"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>The growth of social media networks have also created new opportunities for automated and efficient news gathering for journalists and newsrooms. Many newsrooms (broadcasters, newspapers, magazines, radio and TV) have started to perform <a class="external text" href="https://ezyinsights.com/best-practices-in-news-gathering/" rel="nofollow">news gathering on social media platforms</a>. Social media is creating changes in the consumer behaviour and news consumption. According to a <a class="external text" href="https://www.pewresearch.org/fact-tank/2017/06/12/growth-in-mobile-news-use-driven-by-older-adults/" rel="nofollow">study by Pew Research</a>, a large portion of Americans read news on digital and on mobile devices.
</p><p>Because internet does not have the "column inches" limitation of print media, online news stories can, but don't always, come bundled with supplementary material. The medium of the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a> also enables <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperlinking" title="Hyperlinking">hyperlinking</a>, which allows readers to navigate to other pages related to the one they're reading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_165-4"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite these changes, some studies have concluded that internet news coverage remains fairly homogenous and dominated by news agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watanabe2013_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watanabe2013-181">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paterson2006_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paterson2006-182">[182]</a></sup> And journalists working with online media do not identify significantly different criteria for newsworthiness than print journalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SKH_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-SKH-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="News_agencies">News agencies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: News agencies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/News_agency" title="News agency">News agency</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079071-0007,_Bonn,_Nachrichtenagentur_Reuters.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="783" data-file-width="490" decoding="async" height="352" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079071-0007%2C_Bonn%2C_Nachrichtenagentur_Reuters.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079071-0007%2C_Bonn%2C_Nachrichtenagentur_Reuters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079071-0007%2C_Bonn%2C_Nachrichtenagentur_Reuters.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079071-0007%2C_Bonn%2C_Nachrichtenagentur_Reuters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079071-0007%2C_Bonn%2C_Nachrichtenagentur_Reuters.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079071-0007%2C_Bonn%2C_Nachrichtenagentur_Reuters.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-F079071-0007,_Bonn,_Nachrichtenagentur_Reuters.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reuters office in Bonn, Germany, 1988</div></div></div>
<p>News agencies are services which compile news and disseminate it in bulk. Because they disseminate information to a wide variety of clients, who repackage the material as news for public consumption, news agencies tend to use less controversial language in their reports. Despite their importance, news agencies are not well known by the general public. They keep low profiles and their reporters usually do not get bylines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacGregor_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacGregor-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JohnstonForde_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-JohnstonForde-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest news agency still operating is the <a href="/wiki/Agence_France-Presse" title="Agence France-Presse">Agence France-Presse</a> (AFP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> It was founded in 1835 by a Parisian translator and <a href="/wiki/Advertising_agency" title="Advertising agency">advertising agent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles-Louis_Havas" title="Charles-Louis Havas">Charles-Louis Havas</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agence_Havas" title="Agence Havas">Agence Havas</a>. By the end of the twentieth century, <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a> far outpaced the other news agencies in profits, and became one of the largest companies in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> In 2011, Thomson Reuters employed more than 55,000 people in 100 countries, and posted an annual revenue of $12.9 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacGregor_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-MacGregor-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/United_Press_International" title="United Press International">United Press International</a> gained prominence as a world news agency in the middle of the twentieth century, but shrank in the 1980s and was sold off at low prices. It is owned by the <a href="/wiki/Unification_Church" title="Unification Church">Unification Church</a> company <a href="/wiki/News_World_Communications" title="News World Communications">News World Communications</a>.
</p><p>News agencies, especially Reuters and the newly important <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_News" title="Bloomberg News">Bloomberg News</a>, convey both news stories for mass audiences and financial information of interest to businesses and investors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OBB21_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-OBB21-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ewha_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ewha-187">[187]</a></sup> Bloomberg LP, a private company founded by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Bloomberg" title="Michael Bloomberg">Michael Bloomberg</a> in 1981, made rapid advances with computerized stock market reporting updated in real time. Its news service continued to exploit this electronic advantage by combining computer-generated analytics with text reporting. Bloomberg linked with Agence France Presse in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ewha_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ewha-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the marketization of the Chinese economy and the media boom of the 1990s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xinhua" title="Xinhua">Xinhua</a> has adopted some commercial practices including subscription fees, but it remains government-subsidized. It provides newswire, news photos, economic information, and audio and video news. Xinhua has a growing number of subscribers, totaling 16,969 in 2002, including 93% of Chinese newspapers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XinXin_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-XinXin-135">[135]</a></sup> It operates 123 foreign bureaus and produces 300 news stories each day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other agencies with considerable reach include <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Presse-Agentur" title="Deutsche Presse-Agentur">Deutsche Presse-Agentur</a> (Germany), <a href="/wiki/Kyodo_News" title="Kyodo News">Kyodo News</a> (Japan), the <i><a href="/wiki/Agenzia_Nazionale_Stampa_Associata" title="Agenzia Nazionale Stampa Associata">Agenzia Nazionale Stampa Associata</a></i> (Italy), the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East_News_Agency" title="Middle East News Agency">Middle East News Agency</a> (Egypt), <a href="/wiki/Tanjug" title="Tanjug">Tanjug</a> (Serbia), <a href="/wiki/EFE" title="EFE">EFE</a> (Spain), and <a href="/wiki/Anadolu_Agency" title="Anadolu Agency">Anadolu Agency</a> (Turkey).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the internet, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/News_aggregators" title="News aggregators">news aggregators</a> play a role similar to that of the news agency—and, because of the sources they select, tend to transmit news stories which originate from the main agencies. Of articles displayed by <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!_News" title="Yahoo! News">Yahoo! News</a> in the U.S., 91.7% come from news agencies: 39.4% from AP, 30.9% AFP, and 21.3% Reuters. In India, 60.1% of Yahoo! News stories come from Reuters. <a href="/wiki/Google_News" title="Google News">Google News</a> relies somewhat less on news agencies, and has shown high volatility, in the sense of focusing heavily on the most recent handful of salient world events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watanabe2013_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watanabe2013-181">[181]</a></sup> In 2010, Google News redesigned its front page with automatic geotargeting, which generated a selection of local news items for every viewer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_news_system">Global news system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Global news system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the 20th century, global news coverage was dominated by a combination of the "Big Four" news agencies—Reuters, Associated Press, Agence France Press, and United Press International—representing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_bloc" title="Western bloc">Western bloc</a>, and the Communist agencies: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_News_Agency_%22TASS%22" title='Russian News Agency "TASS"'>TASS</a> from the Soviet Union, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xinhua" title="Xinhua">Xinhua</a> from China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Studies of major world events, and analyses of all international news coverage in various newspapers, consistently found that a large majority of news items originated from the four biggest wire services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watanabe2013_181-2"><a href="#cite_note-Watanabe2013-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>Television news agencies include <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press_Television_News" title="Associated Press Television News">Associated Press Television News</a>, which bought and incorporated World Television News; and Reuters Television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hachten39_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hachten39-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> Bloomberg News created in the 1990s, expanded rapidly to become a player in the realm of international news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OBB21_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-OBB21-186">[186]</a></sup> The Associated Press also maintains a radio network with thousands of subscribers worldwide; it is the sole provider of international news to many small stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hachten39_174-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hachten39-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>By some accounts, dating back to the 1940s, the increasing interconnectedness of the news system has accelerated the pace of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_world" title="History of the world">world history</a> itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_World_Information_and_Communication_Order">New World Information and Communication Order</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: New World Information and Communication Order">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The global news system is dominated by agencies from Europe and the United States, and reflects their interests and priorities in its coverage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Euro-American control of the global news system has led to criticism; that events around the world are constantly compared to events like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a> and World War II, which are considered foundational in the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeiler31_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zeiler31-195">[195]</a></sup> Since the 1960s, a significant amount of news reporting from the Third World has been characterized by some form "development journalism", a paradigm which focuses on long-term development projects, social change, and nation-building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> When in 1987 the U.S. media reported on a riot in the Dominican Republic—the first major news item regarding that country in years—the resulting decline in tourism lasted for years and had a noticeable effect on the economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> The English language predominates in global news exchanges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> Critics have accused the global news system of perpetuating <a href="/wiki/Cultural_imperialism" title="Cultural imperialism">cultural imperialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomlinson_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tomlinson-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SilverblattZlobin28_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-SilverblattZlobin28-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> Critics further charge that the Western media conglomerates maintain a bias towards the status quo economic order, especially a pro-corporate bias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SilverblattZlobin28_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-SilverblattZlobin28-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Educational,_Scientific_and_Cultural_Organization" title="United Nations Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization">United Nations Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization</a> (UNESCO) has promoted a <a href="/wiki/New_World_Information_and_Communication_Order" title="New World Information and Communication Order">New World Information and Communication Order</a>, which envisions an international news exchange system involving national news agencies in every country. UNESCO encouraged the new states formed from colonial territories in the 1960s to establish news agencies, to generate domestic news stories, exchange news items with international partners, and disseminate both types of news internally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Along these lines, the 1980 <a href="/wiki/MacBride_report" title="MacBride report">MacBride report</a>, "Many Voices, One World", called for an interdependent global news system with more participation from different governments. To this end, also, UNESCO formed the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_News_Agencies_Pool" title="Non-Aligned News Agencies Pool">Non-Aligned News Agencies Pool</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Inter_Press_Service" title="Inter Press Service">Inter Press Service</a>, founded in 1964, has served as an intermediary for Third World press agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> Inter Press Service's editorial policy favors coverage of events, institutions, and issues which relate to inequality, <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economic_integration" title="Economic integration">economic integration</a>, natural resources, population, health, education, and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giffard194_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giffard194-204">[204]</a></sup> It gives less coverage than other agencies to crime, disasters, and violence. Geographically, 70% of its news reporting concerns Africa, Asia, Latin America, and the Caribbean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> IPS has the most subscribers in Latin America and southern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giffard194_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-Giffard194-204">[204]</a></sup> IPS receives grants from organizations such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Program" title="United Nations Development Program">United Nations Development Program</a> and other United Nations agencies and private foundations to report news on chosen topics, including the environment, sustainable development, and women's issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in the 1960s, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">United States Agency for International Development</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration" title="National Aeronautics and Space Administration">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a>, and UNESCO developed the use of satellite television for international broadcasting. In India, 1975–1976, these agencies implemented an experimental satellite television system, called the <a href="/wiki/Satellite_Instructional_Television_Experiment" title="Satellite Instructional Television Experiment">Satellite Instructional Television Experiment</a>, with assistance from the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">Indian Space Research Organisation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/All_India_Radio" title="All India Radio">All India Radio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_transformation_in_global_news_flow">Further transformation in global news flow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Further transformation in global news flow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the 1980s, much of the Third World had succumbed to a <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_debt_crisis" title="Latin American debt crisis">debt crisis</a> resulting from unpayable large loans accumulated since the 1960s. At this point, the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> took an active role in the governance of many countries, and its authority extended to communications policy. The policy of developing Third World media gave way to a global regime of <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> institutions like the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>, which also protected the free flow of information across borders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> The World Bank also promoted privatization of national telecommunications, which afforded large multinational corporations the opportunity to purchase networks and expand operations in the Third World.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>In countries with less telecommunications infrastructure, people, especially youth, tend today to get their news predominantly from mobile phones and, less so, from the internet. Older folks listen more to the radio. The government of China is a major investor in Third World telecommunications, especially in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Some issues relating to global information flow were revisited in light of the internet at the 2003/2005 <a href="/wiki/World_Summit_on_the_Information_Society" title="World Summit on the Information Society">World Summit on the Information Society</a>, a conference which emphasized the role of civil society and the private sector in <a href="/wiki/Information_society" title="Information society">information society</a> governance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="News_values">News values</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: News values">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/News_values" title="News values">News values</a></div>
<p>News values are the professional norms of <a href="/wiki/Journalism" title="Journalism">journalism</a>. Commonly, news content should contain all the "<a href="/wiki/Five_Ws" title="Five Ws">Five Ws</a>" (who, what, when, where, why, and also how) of an event. Newspapers normally place hard news stories on the first pages, so the most important information is at the beginning, enabling busy readers to read as little or as much as they desire. Local stations and networks with a set format must take news stories and break them down into the most important aspects due to time constraints.
</p><p>Journalists are often expected to aim for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Objectivity_(journalism)" title="Objectivity (journalism)">objectivity</a>; reporters claim to try to cover all sides of an issue without bias, as compared to commentators or analysts, who provide <a href="/wiki/Opinion" title="Opinion">opinion</a> or personal points of view. The resulting articles lay out facts in a sterile, noncommittal manner, standing back to "let the reader decide" the truth of the matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> Several governments impose certain constraints against bias. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the government agency of <a href="/wiki/Ofcom" title="Ofcom">Ofcom</a> (Office of Communications) enforces a legal requirement of "impartiality" on news broadcasters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-214">[214]</a></sup> Both newspapers and broadcast news programs in the United States are generally expected to remain neutral and avoid bias except for clearly indicated editorial articles or segments. Many single-party governments have operated state-run news organizations, which may present the government's views.
</p><p>Although newswriters have always laid claim to truth and objectivity, the modern values of professional journalism were established beginning in the late 1800s and especially after World War I, when groups like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Newspaper_Editors" title="American Society of Newspaper Editors">American Society of Newspaper Editors</a> codified rules for unbiased news reporting. These norms held the most sway in American and British journalism, and were scorned by some other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> These ideas have become part of the practice of journalism across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> Soviet commentators said stories in the Western press were trivial distractions from reality, and emphasized a <a href="/wiki/Socialist_realism" title="Socialist realism">socialist realism</a> model focusing on developments in everyday life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even in those situations where objectivity is expected, it is difficult to achieve, and individual journalists may fall foul of their own personal bias, or succumb to <a href="/wiki/Media_bias" title="Media bias">commercial or political pressure</a>. Similarly, the objectivity of news organizations owned by conglomerated corporations fairly may be questioned, in light of the natural incentive for such groups to report news in a manner intended to advance the conglomerate's financial interests. Individuals and organizations who are the subject of news reports may use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/News_management" title="News management">news management</a> techniques to try to make a favourable impression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> Because each individual has a particular point of view, it is recognized that there can be no absolute objectivity in news reporting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> Journalists can collectively shift their opinion over what is a controversy up for debate and what is an established fact, as evidenced by homogenization during the 2000s of news coverage of climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some commentators on news values have argued that journalists' training in news values <i>itself</i> represents a systemic bias of the news. The norm of objectivity leads journalists to gravitate towards certain types of acts and exclude others. A journalist can be sure of objectivity in reporting that an official or public figure has made a certain statement. This is one reason why so much news reporting is devoted to official statements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> This lemma dates back to the early history of public news reporting, as exemplified by an English printer who on 12 March 1624 published news from Brussels in the form of letters, with the prefacing comment: "Now because you shall not say, that either out of my owne conceit I misliked a phrase, or presumptuously tooke upon me to reforme any thing amisse, I will truly set you downe their owne words."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Strong_objectivity" title="Strong objectivity">Feminist critiques</a> argue that discourse defined as objective by news organizations reflects a male-centered perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> In their selection of sources, journalists rely heavily on men as sources of authoritative- and objective-seeming statements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> News reporting has also tended to discuss women differently, usually in terms of appearance and relationship to men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>The critique of traditional norms of objectivity comes from within news organizations as well. Said Peter Horrocks, head of television news at BBC: "The days of middle-of-the-road, balancing Left and Right, impartiality are dead. […] we need to consider adopting what I like to think of as a much wider 'radical impartiality'—the need to hear the widest range of views—all sides of the story."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_organization_of_news_production">Social organization of news production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Social organization of news production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="News_organizations">News organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: News organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Viewed from a <a href="/wiki/Sociology_of_knowledge" title="Sociology of knowledge">sociological perspective</a>, news for mass consumption is produced in hierarchical organizations. Reporters, working near the bottom of the structure, are given significant autonomy in researching and preparing reports, subject to assignments and occasional intervention from higher decision-makers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> Owners at the top of the news hierarchy influence the content of news indirectly but substantially. The professional norms of journalism discourage overt censorship; however, news organizations have covert but firm norms about how to cover certain topics. These policies are conveyed to journalists through socialization on the job; without any written policy, they simply learn how things are done.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> Journalists comply with these rules for various reasons, including job security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> Journalists are also systematically influenced by their education, including <a href="/wiki/Journalism_school" title="Journalism school">journalism school</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup>
</p><p>News production is routinized in several ways. News stories use familiar formats and subgenres which vary by topic. "Rituals of objectivity", such as pairing a quotation from one group with a quotation from a competing group, dictate the construction of most news narratives. Many news items revolve around periodic press conferences or other scheduled events. Further routine is established by assigning each journalist to a <a href="/wiki/Beat_reporting" title="Beat reporting">beat</a>: a domain of human affairs, usually involving government or commerce, in which certain types of events routinely occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup>
</p><p>A common scholarly frame for understanding news production is to examine the role of information <a href="/wiki/Gatekeeping_(communication)" title="Gatekeeping (communication)">gatekeepers</a>: to ask why and how certain narratives make their way from news producers to news consumers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> Obvious gatekeepers include journalists, news agency staff, and wire editors of newspapers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> Ideology, personal preferences, source of news, and length of a story are among the many considerations which influence gatekeepers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> Although social media have changed the structure of news dissemination, gatekeeper effects may continue due to the role of a few central nodes in the social network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>New factors have emerged in internet-era newsrooms. One issue is "click-thinking", the editorial selection of news stories—and of journalists—who can generate the most website hits and thus advertising revenue. Unlike a newspaper, a news website has detailed data collection about which stories are popular and who is reading them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JohnstonForde_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-JohnstonForde-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> The drive for speedy online postings, some journalists have acknowledged, has altered norms of fact-checking so that verification takes place after publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JohnstonForde_183-2"><a href="#cite_note-JohnstonForde-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup>
</p><p>Journalists' sometimes unattributed echoing of other news sources can also increase the homogeneity of news feeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-239">[239]</a></sup> The digital age can accelerate the problem of <a href="/wiki/Circular_reporting" title="Circular reporting">circular reporting</a>: propagation of the same error through increasingly reliable sources. In 2009, a number of journalists were embarrassed after all reproducing a fictional quotation, originating from Wikipedia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-239">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup>
</p><p>News organizations have historically been male-dominated, though women have acted as journalists since at least the 1880s. The number of female journalists has increased over time, but organizational hierarchies remain controlled mostly by men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> Studies of British news organizations estimate that more than 80% of decision-makers are men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> Similar studies have found '<a href="/wiki/Old_boy_network" title="Old boy network">old boys' networks</a>' in control of news organizations in the United States and the Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> Further, newsrooms tend to divide journalists by gender, assigning men to "hard" topics like military, crime, and economics, and women to "soft", "humanised" topics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_institutions">Relationship with institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Relationship with institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For various reasons, news media usually have a close relationship with the state, and often church as well, even when they cast themselves in critical roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fang14_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fang14-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephens27_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stephens27-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salmon90_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salmon90-245">[245]</a></sup> This relationship seems to emerge because the press can develop symbiotic relationships with other powerful social institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salmon90_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-Salmon90-245">[245]</a></sup> In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> wire service developed a "bilateral monopoly" with the <a href="/wiki/Western_Union" title="Western Union">Western Union</a> telegraph company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starr177_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Starr177-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup>
</p><p>The news agencies which rose to power in the mid-1800s all had support from their respective governments, and in turn served their political interests to some degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OBB23_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-OBB23-139">[139]</a></sup> News for consumption has operated under <a href="/wiki/Statism" title="Statism">statist</a> assumptions, even when it takes a stance adversarial to some aspect of a government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> In practice, a large proportion of routine news production involves interactions between reporters and government officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> Relatedly, journalists tend to adopt a hierarchical view of society, according to which a few people at the top of organizational pyramids are best situated to comment on the reality which serves as the basisi of news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> Broadly speaking, therefore, news tends to normalize and reflect the interests of the power structure dominant in its social context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, international non-governmental organizations (NGOs) rival and may surpass governments in their influence on the content of news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanLeuvenJoye_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-VanLeuvenJoye-251">[251]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_control">State control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: State control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/State_media" title="State media">State media</a></div>
<p>Governments use international news transmissions to promote the national interest and conduct <a href="/wiki/Political_warfare" title="Political warfare">political warfare</a>, alternatively known as <a href="/wiki/Public_diplomacy" title="Public diplomacy">public diplomacy</a> and, in the modern era, <a href="/wiki/International_broadcasting" title="International broadcasting">international broadcasting</a>. International radio broadcasting came into wide-ranging use by world powers seeking cultural integration of their empires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> The British government used BBC radio as a diplomatic tool, setting up Arabic, Spanish and Portuguese services in 1937.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> American propaganda broadcasters include <a href="/wiki/Voice_of_America" title="Voice of America">Voice of America</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radio_Free_Europe/Radio_Liberty" title="Radio Free Europe/Radio Liberty">Radio Free Europe/Radio Liberty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crusade_for_Freedom" title="Crusade for Freedom">set up during the Cold War</a> and still operating today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> The United States remains the world's top broadcaster, although by some accounts it was surpassed for a time circa 1980 <a href="/wiki/Radio_Moscow" title="Radio Moscow">by the Soviet Union</a>. Other major international broadcasters include the People's Republic of China, Taiwan, Germany, Saudi Arabia, Egypt, North Korea, India, Cuba, and Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup> Around the world (and especially, formerly, in the Soviet bloc), international news sources such as the <a href="/wiki/BBC_World_Service" title="BBC World Service">BBC World Service</a> are often welcomed as alternatives to domestic state-run media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p>Governments have also funneled programming through private news organizations, as when the British government arranged to insert news into the Reuters feed during and after World War Two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> Past revelations have suggested that the U.S. military and intelligence agencies create news stories which they disseminate secretly into the foreign and domestic media. Investigation into the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> pursued in the 1970s found that it owned hundreds of news organizations (wire services, newspapers, magazines) outright.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> Soviet news warfare also involved the creation of front groups, like the <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_of_Journalists" title="International Organization of Journalists">International Organization of Journalists</a>. The Russian <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a> heavily pursued a strategy of <a href="/wiki/Disinformation" title="Disinformation">disinformation</a>, planting false stories which made their way to news outlets worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup>
</p><p>Broadcasts into Iraq before the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Second Gulf War</a> mimicked the style of local programming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> The US also launched Middle East Broadcasting Networks, featuring the satellite TV station <a href="/wiki/Alhurra" title="Alhurra">Alhurra</a> and radio station <a href="/wiki/Radio_Sawa" title="Radio Sawa">Radio Sawa</a> to beam 24-hour programming to Iraq and environs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, <a href="/wiki/Al_Jazeera" title="Al Jazeera">Al Jazeera</a>, a TV and internet news network owned by the government of <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, has become one of the foremost news sources in the world, appreciated by millions as an alternative to the Western media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup> State-owned <a href="/wiki/China_Central_Television" title="China Central Television">China Central Television</a> operates 18 channels and reaches more than a billion viewers worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> Iran's <a href="/wiki/Press_TV" title="Press TV">Press TV</a> and Russia's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russia_Today" title="Russia Today">Russia Today</a>, branded as RT, also have multiplatform presences and large audiences.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_relations">Public relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Public relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:39%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>If important things of life to-day consist of trans-atlantic radiophone talks arranged by commercial telephone companies; if they consist of inventions that will be commercially advantageous to the men who market them; if they consist of Henry Fords with epoch-making cars—then all this is news.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/Edward_Bernays" title="Edward Bernays">Edward Bernays</a>, <i>Propaganda</i> (1928), pp. 152–153.</cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>As distinct from <a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">advertising</a>, which deals with marketing distinct from news, <a href="/wiki/Public_relations" title="Public relations">public relations</a> involves the techniques of influencing news in order to give a certain impression to the public. A standard public relations tactic, the "third-party technique", is the creation of seemingly independent organizations, which can deliver objective-sounding statements to news organizations without revealing their corporate connections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> Public relations agencies can create complete content packages, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_News_Release" title="Video News Release">Video News Releases</a>, which are rebroadcast as news without commentary or detail about their origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup> Video news releases seem like normal news programming, but use subtle <a href="/wiki/Product_placement" title="Product placement">product placement</a> and other techniques to influence viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup>
</p><p>Public relations releases offer valuable newsworthy information to increasingly overworked journalists on deadline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_239-2"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-239">[239]</a></sup> (This pre-organized news content has been called an <a href="/wiki/Information_subsidy" title="Information subsidy">information subsidy</a>.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup> The journalist relies on appearances of autonomy and even opposition to established interests—but the public relations agent seek to conceal their client's influence on the news,. Thus, public relations works its magic in secret.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanLeuvenJoye_251-1"><a href="#cite_note-VanLeuvenJoye-251">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoloneyJacksonMcQueen_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-MoloneyJacksonMcQueen-270">[270]</a></sup>
</p><p>Public relations can dovetail with state objectives, as in the case of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nayirah_(testimony)" title="Nayirah (testimony)">1990 news story</a> about Iraqi soldiers taking "babies out of incubators" in Kuwaiti hospitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Civil_War" title="Nigerian Civil War">Nigerian Civil War</a>, both the federal government and the secessionist Republic of Biafra hired public relations firms, which competed to influence public opinion in the West, and between them established some of the key narratives employed in news reports about the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup>
</p><p>Overall, the position of the public relations industry has grown stronger, while the position of news producers has grown weaker. Public relations agents mediate the production of news about all sectors of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoloneyJacksonMcQueen_270-1"><a href="#cite_note-MoloneyJacksonMcQueen-270">[270]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="News_consumption">News consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: News consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Over the centuries, commentators on newspapers and society have repeatedly observed widespread human interest in news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephens13_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stephens13-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> Elite members of a society's political and economic institutions might rely on news as one limited source of information, for the masses, news represents a relatively exclusive window onto the operations by which a society is managed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regular people in societies with news media often spend a lot of time reading or watching news reports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup> Newspapers became significant aspects of national and literary culture—as exemplified by James Joyce's <i><a href="/wiki/Ulysses_(novel)" title="Ulysses (novel)">Ulysses</a></i>, which derives from the newspapers of 16 June (and thereabouts), 1904, and represents the newspaper office itself as a vital part of life in Dublin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 1945 study by sociologist <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Berelson" title="Bernard Berelson">Bernard Berelson</a> found that during the 1945 New York newspaper strike, New Yorkers exhibited a virtual addiction to news, describing themselves as "lost", "nervous", "isolated", and "suffering" due to the withdrawal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup> Television news has become still further embedded in everyday life, with specific programming anticipated at different times of day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen47_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen47-278">[278]</a></sup> Children tend to find the news boring, too serious, or emotionally disturbing. They come to perceive news as characteristic of adulthood, and begin watching television news in their teenage years because of the adult status it confers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup>
</p><p>People exhibit various forms of skepticism towards the news. Studies of <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_(newspaper_format)" title="Tabloid (newspaper format)">tabloid</a> readers found that many of them gain pleasure from seeing through the obviously fake or poorly constructed stories—and get their "real news" from television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_cultural_cohesion">Social and cultural cohesion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Social and cultural cohesion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An important feature distinguishing news from private information transfers is the impression that when one reads (or hears, or watches) it, one joins a larger public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starr24_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starr24-281">[281]</a></sup> In this regard news serves to unify its receivers under the banner of a culture, or a society, as well as into the sub-demographics of a society targeted by their favorite kind of news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup> News thus plays a role in <a href="/wiki/Nation-building" title="Nation-building">nation-building</a>, the construction of a national identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Photojournalism" title="Photojournalism">Images connected with news</a> can also become iconic and gain a fixed role in the culture. Examples such as <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Eisenstaedt" title="Alfred Eisenstaedt">Alfred Eisenstaedt</a>'s photograph <i><a href="/wiki/V-J_Day_in_Times_Square" title="V-J Day in Times Square">V-J Day in Times Square</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Nick_Ut" title="Nick Ut">Nick Ut</a>'s photograph of <a href="/wiki/Phan_Thi_Kim_Phuc" title="Phan Thi Kim Phuc">Phan Thi Kim Phuc</a> and other children running from a napalm blast in Vietnam; <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Carter" title="Kevin Carter">Kevin Carter</a>'s photograph of a starving child being stalked by a vulture;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeiler31_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zeiler31-195">[195]</a></sup> etc.
</p><p>With the new interconnectedness of global media, the experience of receiving news along with a world audience reinforces the social cohesion effect on a larger scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> As a corollary, global media culture may erode the uniqueness and cohesion of national cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SilverblattZlobin28_199-2"><a href="#cite_note-SilverblattZlobin28-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_sphere">Public sphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Public sphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This collective form experience can be understood to constitute a political realm or <a href="/wiki/Public_sphere" title="Public sphere">public sphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starr24_281-1"><a href="#cite_note-Starr24-281">[281]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup> In this view, the news media constitute a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourth_estate" title="Fourth estate">fourth estate</a> which serves to check and balance the operations of government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen47_278-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allen47-278">[278]</a></sup>
</p><p>This idea, at least as a goal to be sought, has re-emerged in the era of global communications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup> Today, unprecedented opportunities exist for public analysis and discussion of world events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup> According to one interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/CNN_effect" title="CNN effect">CNN effect</a>, instantaneous global news coverage can rally public opinion as never before to motivate political action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup> In 1989, local and global communications media-enabled instant exposure to and discussion of the Chinese government's actions in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiananmen_Square_protests_of_1989" title="Tiananmen Square protests of 1989">Tiananmen Square</a>. The news about Tiananmen Square travelled over a fax machine, telephone, newspaper, radio, and television, and continued to travel even after the government imposed new restrictions on local telecommunications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="News_events">News events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: News events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As the technological means for disseminating news grew more powerful, news became an experience which millions of people could undergo simultaneously. Outstanding news experiences can exert a profound influence on millions of people. Through its power to effect a shared experience, news events can mold the <a href="/wiki/Collective_memory" title="Collective memory">collective memory</a> of a society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup>
</p><p>One type of news event, the <a href="/wiki/Media_event" title="Media event">media event</a>, is a scripted pageant organized for a mass live broadcast. Media events include athletic contests such as the Super Bowl and the Olympics, cultural events like awards ceremonies and celebrity funerals, and also political events such as coronations, debates between electoral candidates, and diplomatic ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup> These events typically unfold according to a common format which simplifies the transmission of news items about them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[293]</a></sup> Usually, they have the effect of increasing the perceived unity of all parties involved, which include the broadcasters and audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup> Today, international events such as a national declaration of independence can be scripted in advance with the major news agencies, with staff specially deployed to key locations worldwide in advance of the life news broadcast. Public relations companies can participate in these events as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup>
</p><p>The perception that an ongoing crisis is taking place further increases the significance of live news. People rely on the news and constantly seek more of it, to learn new information and to seek reassurance amidst feelings of fear and uncertainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup> Crises can also increase the effect of the news on social cohesion, and lead the population of a country to "rally" behind its current leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup> The rise of a global news system goes hand in hand with the advent of <a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">terrorism</a> and other sensational acts, which have power in proportion to the audience they capture. In 1979, the <a href="/wiki/Iran_hostage_crisis" title="Iran hostage crisis">capture of American hostages in Iran</a> dominated months of news coverage in the western media, gained the status of a "crisis", and influenced a presidential election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup>
</p><p>South Africans overwhelmingly describe the end of Apartheid as a source of the country's most important news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup> In the United States, news events such as the assassinations of the 1960s (of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="The assassination of John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." title="Assassination of Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of Robert F. Kennedy">Robert F. Kennedy</a>), the 1969 <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">moon landing</a>, the 1986 Challenger explosion, the 1997 death of Princess Diana, the <a href="/wiki/Bush_v._Gore" title="Bush v. Gore">intervention of the Supreme Court</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2000_United_States_presidential_election" title="2000 United States presidential election">2000 presidential election</a> and the 2001 <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup> In Jordan, people cited numerous memorable news events involving death and war, including the death of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Hussein" title="King Hussein">King Hussein</a>, Princess Diana, and <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Yitzhak_Rabin" title="Assassination of Yitzhak Rabin">Yitzhak Rabin</a>. Positive news stories found memorable by Jordanians featured political events affecting their lives and families—such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Lebanon_security_belt" title="South Lebanon security belt">Israeli withdrawal from South Lebanon</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Israel%E2%80%93Jordan_peace_treaty" title="Israel–Jordan peace treaty">Israel–Jordan peace treaty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[301]</a></sup>
</p><p>News coverage can also shape collective memory in retrospect. A study of Israeli news coverage leading up to the media event of the nation's 60th birthday found that news coverage of events like the Holocaust, World War Two, and subsequent Israeli wars increased the perceived importance of these events in the minds of citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[302]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="News_making">News making</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: News making">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>News making is the act of making the news or doing something that is considered to be newsworthy. When discussing the act of news making, scholars refer to specific models. Five of these models are the Professional Model, Mirror Model, Organizational Model, Political Model, and Civic Journalism Model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graber_1980_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graber_1980-303">[303]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Professional Model is when skilled peoples put certain events together for a specific audience. The reaction of the audience is influential because it can determine the impact that the particular article or newspaper has on the readers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-post.queensu.ca_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-post.queensu.ca-304">[304]</a></sup> The Mirror Model states that news should reflect reality. This model aims to focus on particular events and provide accuracy in reporting. The Organizational Model is also known as the Bargaining Model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graber_1980_303-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graber_1980-303">[303]</a></sup> It focuses on influencing various news organizations by applying pressures to governmental processes. The Political Model outlines that news represents the ideological biases of the people as well as the various pressures of the political environment. This model mainly influences journalists and attempts to promote public opinion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-post.queensu.ca_304-1"><a href="#cite_note-post.queensu.ca-304">[304]</a></sup> The Civic Journalism Model is when the press discovers the concerns of the people and uses that to write stories. This allows the audience to play an active role in society.
</p><p>Models of news making help define what the news is and how it influences readers. But it does not necessarily account for the content of print news and online media. Stories are selected if they have a strong impact, incorporate violence and scandal, are familiar and local, and if they are timely.
</p><p>News Stories with a strong impact can be easily understood by a reader. Violence and scandal create an entertaining and attention-grabbing story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graber_1980_303-2"><a href="#cite_note-Graber_1980-303">[303]</a></sup> Familiarity makes a story more relatable because the reader knows who is being talked about. Proximity can influence a reader more. A story that is timely will receive more coverage because it is a current event. The process of selecting stories coupled with the models of news making are how the media is effective and impactful in society.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychological_effects">Psychological effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Psychological effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Media_influence" title="Media influence">Media influence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultivation_theory" title="Cultivation theory">Cultivation theory</a></div>
<p>Exposure to constant news coverage of war can lead to stress and anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup> Television coverage of the <a href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_World_Trade_Center" title="Collapse of the World Trade Center">destruction of the World Trade Center</a> in 2001, which repeated the same footage over and over, led to symptoms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trauma_(medicine)" title="Trauma (medicine)">trauma</a> experienced across the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup> Studies have indicated that children have been traumatized by exposure to television of other frightening events, including the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">Challenger disaster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup> Journalists themselves also experience trauma and guilt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research also suggest that constant representations of violence in the news lead people to overestimate the frequency of its occurrence in the real world, thus increasing their level of fear in everyday situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[309]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The content and style of news delivery certainly have effects on the general public, with the magnitude and precise nature of these effects being tough to determine experimentally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup> In Western societies, television viewing has been so ubiquitous that its total effect on psychology and culture leave few alternatives for comparison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[311]</a></sup>
</p><p>News is the leading source of knowledge about global affairs for people around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[312]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Agenda-setting_theory" title="Agenda-setting theory">agenda-setting theory</a>, the general public will identify as its priorities those issues which are highlighted on the news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[313]</a></sup> The agenda-setting model has been well-supported by research, which indicate that the public's self-reported concerns respond to changes in news coverage rather than changes in the underlying issue itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[314]</a></sup> The less an issue obviously affects people's lives, the bigger an influence media agenda-setting can have on their opinion of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[315]</a></sup> The agenda-setting power becomes even stronger in practice because of the correspondence in news topics promulgated by different media channels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[316]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_of_sponsorship">Influence of sponsorship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=News&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Influence of sponsorship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It has been acknowledged that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sponsorship" title="Sponsorship">sponsorship</a> has historically influenced various news stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marketrevealed_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-marketrevealed-317">[317]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theweekreports_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-theweekreports-318">[318]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-startribunereports_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-startribunereports-319">[319]</a></sup> This history gained widespread attention following the release of the film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anchorman_2" title="Anchorman 2">Anchorman 2</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marketrevealed_317-1"><a href="#cite_note-marketrevealed-317">[317]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theweekreports_318-1"><a href="#cite_note-theweekreports-318">[318]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-startribunereports_319-1"><a href="#cite_note-startribunereports-319">[319]</a></sup> One example in recent time is  the fact that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meta,_Inc" title="Meta, Inc">Facebook</a> has invested heavily in news sources and purchasing time on local news media outlets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-facebookbust_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-facebookbust-320">[320]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbcreports_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-nbcreports-321">[321]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/TechCrunch" title="TechCrunch">TechCrunch</a></i> journalist Josh Continue even stated in February 2018 that the company "stole the news business" and used sponsorship to make many news publishers its "ghostwriters."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-facebookbust_320-1"><a href="#cite_note-facebookbust-320">[320]</a></sup> In January 2019, founder Mark Zuckerberg announced that he will spend $300 million in local news buys over a three-year period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbcreports_321-1"><a href="#cite_note-nbcreports-321">[321]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup>
</p>

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Toronto_School_of_communication_theory" title="Toronto School of communication theory">Toronto School of communication theory</a></li></ul>
